Key factors include:\n\n1. Non-covalent interactions: These are the primary forces stabilizing host-guest complexes and include hydrogen bonding, van der Waals forces, π-π stacking, electrostatic (ionic) interactions, hydrophobic effects, and sometimes metal coordination.\n\n2. Complementarity: The geometric and electronic fit between the host and guest, often described as 'lock-and-key' or 'induced fit', is crucial. Shape, size, and charge complementarity maximize interaction strength and selectivity.\n\n3. Solvent effects: The nature of the solvent can enhance or weaken host-guest binding by competing for interactions or stabilizing/desolvating the guest or host.\n\n4. Thermodynamics: The overall free energy change (ΔG) upon complex formation, which includes enthalpic (ΔH, strength of interactions) and entropic (ΔS, changes in order/disorder) contributions, determines the stability of the complex.\n\n5. Environmental conditions: Factors such as temperature, pH, and ionic strength can modulate the strength and specificity of host-guest interactions.\n\n6.
